B001DE29SS|5.0|Today we know Robert Downey Jr for playing Tony Stark in the \\"Iron Man\\" movies or Sherlock Holmes in the new action blockbuster franchise but before all that he had a subdued yet close to brilliant portrayal as the legendary Charlie Chaplin, who was in his day bigger and more popular than most of our current A list actors and actresses. Some say Chaplin was the first actor to open up the world market and the isolating affect it had on him is explored in this film. Incidentally, Chaplin was considered the most famous film star worldwide during World War I.<br /><br />This 1992 film explores Chaplin's rise to stardom going from acting troupes to taking a job in America in silent films and his gradual control over later films (some not so popular at the time like \\"The Great Dictator\\"), as well as his turbulent romances/marriages (especially the ugly and controversial paternity lawsuit a la Joan Barry) to finally finding the right one. We also get a look into his many pictorial roles, his co-founding of United Artists as well as how McCarthyism ended up fracturing his career and having him move to Switzerland for the next several decades.<br /><br />There's a nice finale to Chaplin's life in which he returns to the 1972 Academy Awards to receive a special award and discovers that even though Hollywood has changed greatly it never forgot him. One of the last of the clips to be shown is a kid being taken away from his father in which Chaplin is arguably demonstrating the fantasy of what he wished for when he was a child. That was a nice wraparound to one of the matters which haunted Chaplin's past . . . being an orphan and abandoned by his parents. I believe the actual picture was the 1921 \\"The Kid\\".<br /><br />Downey's acting got him a nomination for Best Actor by the Golden Globes and the Academy Awards but BAFTA gave him the actual award for Best Actor. The film was nominated by those three for other awards (too many to list, really). Note that one of Chaplin's daughters, Geraldine Chaplin, played the role of Charlie Chaplin's \\"going insane\\" mother in the movie.<br /><br />A number of other actors and actresses played various roles, including Dan Akroyd (Hollywood producer Mack Sennett), Moira Kelly (two roles: Chaplin's first serious love interest and then his final wife), Diane Lane (Chaplin's third wife and actress, Paulette Goddard), Milla Jovovich (another actress wife to Chaplin), Anthony Hopkins (as Chaplin's later editor for his autobiography), Penelope Ann Miller (Hollywood actress), Marisa Tomei (another Hollywood actress), Kevin Kline (as Chaplin's friend, Dougla Fairbanks, not to be confused with Douglas Fairbanks Jr, his son), James Woods and David Duchovny (Chaplin's cameraman). B004LWZWGK|4.0|As soon as \\"The Dark Knight Rises\\" begins, you're practically guaranteed to have goosebumps. The opening scene is hair-raising; it's exciting, it's blood-pumping, it gives you an idea of what to expect over the next two hours and forty four minutes. You're practically tossed around, choked out, and physically manhandled by Bane nearly every moment he's on-screen. No, he's not as big as he was in the comics but he's still pretty freaking big. Tom Hardy is sensational. His eyes are so expressive, angry, and cold. Even though his actions will probably be the first thing you remember about the character, his eyes will practically haunt you during his performance.<br /><br />The entire cast is really phenomenal though. Christian Bale is still a solid Bruce Wayne/Batman, but it certainly seems as though he's more of a foundation for the rest of the cast to be greater than he is. Anne Hathaway is mesmerizing as Selina Kyle. Her being cast in the role seemed a bit underwhelming at first, but she surprises you. She makes the Catwoman character her own; she's intelligent, sexy, always has something quick-witted to say, and you figure out just enough about her to want her to be around more often than she is.<br /><br />The direction the Alfred character goes in is really interesting. He just wants what's best for Bruce, but does something that is easily the most difficult decision of his entire life. Michael Caine is extraordinary. He plays the father figure so perfectly. Gary Oldman shines, as well. Oldman is pretty great in every role he has anyway, but this is the first time in one of the Batman films where he's had to defend his actions. Oldman is so absorbing and enticing whenever he loses his cool or gets angry in a film. Newcomer Joseph Gordon-Levitt also deserves some recognition. Constantly called a hothead by his superior, Jim Gordon takes him under his wing. The John Blake character is so passionate about what he thinks is right and what he thinks Batman should be doing and Joseph Gordon-Levitt does a fantastic job portraying that.<br /><br />Hans Zimmer's soundtrack for the film is easily one of the best this year has had to offer. There's enough familiarity in the soundtrack to trigger nostalgic moments from the two previous films, but it adds something raw and visceral during the chants and during anything that plays in the background when Bane is on screen. A song like \\"The Fire Rises\\" is haunting in a way and the music is so sharp; it cuts to your very bones. Pounding drums, horns that seem to bide their time and start low and then scream when you least expect it, and strings played so vigorously that you expect to hear them snap and break at some point. Meanwhile, the slower songs like \\"Mind If I Cut In\\" rely on something like a simple piano arrangement but also seem kind of playful at the same time to adhere to Selina Kyle.<br /><br />There are certain things in \\"The Dark Knight Rises\\" that make it really solid and one of the best third installments of any franchise, if not the best; the main one being the last twenty minutes or so. The conclusion is spectacular and it seems like Christopher Nolan built this entire near-three-hour spectacle around these key moments. It is near perfect and satisfying in almost every way.<br /><br />The other thing is how Nolan was able to bring so much back from not only \\"The Dark Knight,\\" but also \\"Batman Begins\\" and makes this feel that much more conclusive. Characters you remember are mentioned, Bruce's childhood is brought up on more than one occasion, and the whole movie is just littered with references and throwbacks to what this franchise has already accomplished. It feels like everything comes full circle and that this chapter of Batman is indeed over.<br /><br />Everything in between the jaw-dropping opening and the amazing conclusion feels so familiar though. There are new characters while old ones are taken down new paths and the destruction being done to Gotham is on the biggest scale this universe has ever seen, but if it wasn't for the well-written screenplay it would feel a bit repetitive. As you play the movie over in your head after it ends (and you will, more than once), you begin to see things and wonder why they weren't expanded on.<br /><br />One of them being that Selina Kyle is terrified of Bane. She's obviously familiar with what he's done in the past or had some sort of run-in with him. Their past is never really fully explained and the two characters don't really have any screen time together. Then there's the obvious nitpick of not being fully able to understand Bane at all times. It'll just give you an excuse to see it again, honestly.<br /><br />\\"The Dark Knight Rises\\" is final in the best of ways. It is overflowing with explosive action, features passionate performances from every part of the cast, and is just completely and totally engrossing from beginning to end. Some are calling it a masterpiece, but it doesn't quite reach that status. \\"The Dark Knight Rises\\" is really quite good and even great at times, but doesn't obtain the same level of brilliance its predecessor did.est scale this universe has ever seen, but if it wasn't for the well-written screenplay it would feel a bit repetitive. 0439023491|5.0|Reading this book made me realize how much thought goes into writing such a brilliant book with a brilliant storyline.<br /><br />This book is not about The Hunger Games anymore, it is not even about a romance, or about District 12. This book is about rebellion, revolting against a despicable and vile government, about all 12 districts joining hands to overthrow their leaders and take back what is theirs - basic needs, human rights and so on.<br /><br />In that sense, it made me think a little too much about the \\"Arab Spring\\" and all the different countries revolting after only one man set himself on fire. Very fitting too, as this second installment of \\"The Hunger Games\\" trilogy is called \\"Catching Fire\\". In Katniss's case, all it took is her seeming act of rebellion when she almost took her own life with the poisonous berries.<br /><br />President Snow of the country of Panem now seeks revenge. His methods of revenge are beyond contempt and even begin to stir his own people of the Capitol. Yes, Collins could have gone into more detail, moved the events quickly and so on, but I thought the first half of the book with Katniss being back home and showing us the dynamics of her new everyday life was very important to set up the rest of the book and what is yet to come in the third and final installment. The slow build up to a full-blown revolution is extremely crucial, and Collins did a great job introducing different characters throughout, giving us bits and pieces of what will come, throwing in hints and clues and feeding Katniss and Peeta new pieces of information for them to use in the war to come (p.s. District 13 plays a huge role in all of it).<br /><br />This book has a bit of everything, but at the core of it all is that cry for help. The cry that pleads so save humanity. 140131046X|5.0|Mitch Albom got his start as a sports writer, but he's better known to the general reader as the author of the inspirational books Tuesdays With Morrie and The Five People You Meet in Heaven, both of which were made into TV movies.  Have a Little Faith is in the same genre as Tuesdays With Morrie.<br /><br />Albom grew up in suburban New Jersey, where his family is actively involved in a local synagogue.  Since leaving home, Albom has not actively practiced his faith, but the rabbi, Albert Lewis, asked Albom to do his eulogy.  That led Albom to spend hours and hours with Lewis over the next several years.  As their friendship grows, so does Albom's appreciation for the rabbi he knew as a child but never really knew.<br /><br />Lewis served this congregation for generations; it's the only placed he ever served.  Albom's reports of their time spent together bring out Lewis's wisdom and teaching in such a way that make the reader long to sit at his feet and listen to stories.  Their conversations range widely, encompassing questions big and little.  As a Jewish man married to a Christian, Albom is especially interested in the question of inter-religious relations.  Lewis tells the story of an early episode in his tenure.  After an ugly encounter between a priest from the parish next door and one of the synagogue members, the priest (at the insistence of his superior) and Rabbi Lewis walked arm in arm around the parish schoolyard, during recess, in a demonstration of the fact that the two faiths can coexist.  Albom asked Lewis, \\"But what if someone from another faith won't recognize yours?  Or wants you dead for it?\\"  Lewis sagely replied, \\"That is not faith.  That is hate.\\"<br /><br />Lewis always had a ready answer, or at least a question to make the questioner reflect.  Full of wit, always singing, and loved by all, Lewis left a deep impression on Albom and everyone with whom he came into contact.  Lewis did not hold back when Albom asked him the secret to his happiness: \\"Be satisfied.  Be grateful.  For what you have.  For the love you receive.  And for what God has given you.  That's it.\\"<br /><br />During the time Albom was meeting with Lewis, he also met Henry Covington, pastor of Pilgrim Church and director of I am My Brother's Keeper in Detroit.  This inner city ministry, a church and homeless outreach, was struggling, poor, and small, but full of faith.  Covington's story, though very different from Lewis's, inspired Albom in different ways.  Covington had been in and out of prison, immersed in drugs and crime, when he became a Christian and began serving others.  Albom initially met Covington to investigate the ministry, to determine whether his foundation would give them a grant.  He got to know Covington, visiting him on several occasions and attending services at the church.  He eventually wrote a newspaper column about their having the heat turned off because of their failure to pay the gas bill.  It didn't help that the roof of the old church had a gaping hole.  That little bit of publicity brought interest and money to the ministry, and now the church has a solid roof! (Read about that here.)<br /><br />Albom's storytelling skill and sensitive portrayals of Lewis and Covington draw the reader into a rare circle of friendship and shared faith.  Have a Little Faith reminds us of the treasures that can be found talking with the regular people around us.  We might be inspired by the biographies of great leaders or historical figures, but these two clergymen, common men by the standards of history, possess and convey the kind of greatness that really matters, impacting the lives of people around them.  There are volumes to be written about our neighbors, the family in the pew behind us in church, the family who runs the dry cleaners I go to, the bus driver who picks up my daughter every day.  I know those books will never be written, but Albom reminds me that if I take time to listen, I might hear some stories worth remembering and will likely learn a few things about living life.membering and will likely learn a few things about living life.

B004LWZW8I|2.0|One year after Clash of the Titans and Hollywood is at full throttle cranking out action movies set in mythological Greece and before.  2011 saw the release of a remake of Conan, and this film by Indian director Tarsem Singh.  The movie follows the life of Theseus, a mortal man favored by the gods, as he leads the resistance against evil king Hyperion who is intent on freeing the Titans upon Earth.  First of, this movie bears no resemblance to the Greek myths surrounding the main character.  For those who don't know, the mythical Theseus was known for slaying the Minotaur.  No Minotaur in this movie.  However, there is a lot of bloody, gory action with swords, pikes, arrows and other weapons of hand-to-hand combat.  Some of it looks authentic but most of it looks overly choreographed.  Like just about every other Hollywood creation about ancient times, most of the key characters are played by actors and actresses from former colonies of Britain.  Is it too much to ask for Greek actors and actresses to play some of the roles?  Maybe it is.  The redeeming feature of the movie was its incredible use of colors to create an environment that looked both mythical yet fantastic at the same time.  Whether it was the grayish titans fighting the white-clothed gods amongst the brownish interior of Mount Tartarus, or the dresses of the Oracles, Tarsem Singh always delivers a visual spectacle regardless of what is happening on-screen.  For sure the cinematography looked much more realistic and plausible than in the film 300.  In my final judgement, this movie is OK to watch at home, though not really worth the purchase price of either the DVD or Blu-Ray.

0307744434|4.0|NO SPOILERS HERE:<br /><br />Erin Morganstern has written a wonderful debut novel, but it is not for all.<br /><br />\\"The Night Circus\\" is a romantic novel in both senses of the term: it is a tale of compulsive passion, and it is a story concerning itself with characters and events that are larger than life, a narrative of a complicated relationship between two people who are locked in a competition forced upon them by two cruel masters.<br /><br />And it is in this sense that the reviews here seem to be divided.  I think it comes down to this: if you love romance, and you are willing to be charmed, you will love this book.  If you demand more factual, detail-oriented stories, you will not care much for it.<br /><br />For the romantics out there, those who like their fantasy to be filled with rich, prosaic sentences and suggestions rather than details, then \\"The Night Circus\\" is a book for them.  The titular circus, Les Cirque des Reves, is a festival for the lonely hearts that is like no other, a world unto itself.  While within its wide-ranging tents one can find the typical elements of any circus: trapeze artists, fortune tellers, a contortionists, magicians, in this carnival there is so much more...<br /><br />But Les Cirque des Reves is a pageant of real magic, of enchantment so authentic that no two visits are ever alike.  It is a celebration of beauty and charm: where one tent might let you travel in a maze of almost infinite variation, and the next tent may allow you to recall memories of your past so vivid you will feel like you are there inside them.  And in this sense it s Ms. Morganstern who is the magician here, continually displaying her own gift at writing beautifully captivating phrases and sentences that compel the reader onward.<br /><br />But there are some readers who have taken issue with incomplete characterization, or flimsy plot developments that are light on details and depend instead upon suggestion and nuance, on creating a verdant dreaminess that doesn't work well for those who would like something more physical to sink their teeth into.<br /><br />For instance, though I enjoyed the book I did take issue with the contest itself, set up by two elderly magicians who may or may not be immortal (it's never clear) but have spent their days setting up competitions that depend upon their training of two young prestidigitators who will spend their lives attempting to one up each other to appease their masters, with no ability to end it in any other way beyond their death.<br /><br />WHY, is never really explained.  What gives them the right, one might ask, to create two sorcerers who exist in effect solely to slave away to their masters' whims, and indeed will do so for their whole lives without learning ANYTHING about the rules of the completion or the way a winner will be detected or the eventual outcome of the loser or even the slightest details about past competitions, etc., etc.<br /><br />And, though I liked the book, that particularly rankled me.  Why wouldn't either one of the main characters DEMAND to know more?  Clever writers always make their characters ask things EXACTLY SO, in order that only a certain amount of info gets out, ending the speech at a tense sentence or an adroit phrase that seems to have a particularly satisfying ring to it, even though a real conversation wouldn't do that.  Again, those who enjoy the prose and the cleverness of nuance will enjoy the book much more than those who want more meat upon their bones<br /><br />As for the construction of the book itself, the chapters are extremely brief, which is extremely satisfying and moves the story along quite quickly.  This is a bit of a chimera as well, for adjacent chapters may be a year or more apart, forward or backward in time, or even several countries apart.  It takes a while to catch on to this, and this can be a bit of a problem for some as well, as it is singularly difficult to trace a character's development through time when time itself is jumbled.<br /><br />Still, I liked \\"The Night Circus.\\"  The problems I have mentioned were not difficult for me particularly, but I have seen that they were for some of the reviewers here on Amazon.  As I've said, however,  I think it is YOUR approach to the novel that matters most.  If you're a stickler for detail, and you want to have your plots follow clear, chronological, well-defined steps, this is probably not a book for you.<br /><br />But if you like romance, and the trappings that come with it--the willing suspension of disbelief, a propensity to accept sensuality over substantive detail, a story that depends more on fantasy than fact--then you'll love \\"The Night Circus.\\"  The novel is like walking through a book-length dream.<br /><br />Morganstern is a really good writer.  I liked her world...I thought the circus sounded like a great make-believe world, a place where the quixotic would like to go, where the various tents of the circus itself offered escapes that the true romantic would like to visit...one tent is a fully realized ice garden, another is full of scents so realistic and intimately detailed that one visitor would never  experience what another would, another tent ruled over by a conjurer so talented that the real trick is not in hiding how the feat is pulled off, but in disguising that it is in fact sorcery: real magic DISGUISING itself as a clever trick.<br /><br />This is, after all, the crux of the matter.  For if you are one who does enjoy the fanciful, the ingenuity of wordplay and the actions of larger-than-life characters and events...if you yearn for the cleverness of style and syntax over carefully wrought plot points, then this novel is indeed for you.ime when time itself is jumbled.<br /><br />Still, I liked \\"The Night Circus.\\"  The problems I have mentioned were not difficult for me particularly, but I have seen that they were for some of the reviewers here on Amazon. 0062183591|5.0|Behavioral psychology is a popular topic for non-fiction books today, and it often seems that what can be observed has already been observed.  Everything from confirmation bias to loss aversion to anchoring is covered in numerous wonderful tomes of anecdotes and studies that demonstrate the irrational ways human beings act contrary to what we would expect.  With such a body of work by Ariely and others, I wondered how this book could tell a new story.<br /><br />I was not only pleasantly surprised but excited to discover that Ariely has broken new ground, this time discussing dishonesty, specifically why we cheat  and how we justify it to ourselves.  Ariely is not talking about con-men or compulsive liars, but the little lies everybody tells throughout their lives.  While he starts with the story of Enron, he suddenly makes it clear how these lessons apply to everyday life.<br /><br />Through what he calls the SMORC or Simple Model of Rational Crime, he comes up with a metric to measure just how dishonest we actually can be.  The fascinating thing he discovered is that you can quantify dishonesty.  Most people don't want to outright lie, but they are willing to \\"stretch the truth\\" by a small by definitely measurable percentage when they think nobody is looking.  Through studies using self-graded math tests, Ariely could ascertain in numerous different circumstances just how honest people would actually perform.  Ariely even discusses self-deception, or how when the circumstances are right, we can lie to ourselves.<br /><br />The book is not full of jargon or technical speak.  As with most in the genre, it is light-heartened, entertaining, and fun to read.  You will discover as you read places you might be engaged in active self-deception, and awareness of these acts is the first step towards righting them.  In fact, much of behavioral psychology focuses on things we do without even realizing it.  But through realization, we can start to fix these problems.  In that, Ariely's book is not only an interesting and entertaining read, but a good start in self-improvement for everybody who sometimes fudge the truth.Ariely's book is not only an interesting and entertaining read, but a good start in self-improvement for everybody who sometimes fudge the truth.

0316023744|3.0|This is generally a good book that is well researched and fairly presented, but there are a few shortcomings worth pointing out. The narrative at times loses steam and is reduced to reporting which generals reached which river with which division on which date. General readers will wonder what the significance of these events are and military buffs will wonder why the type of equipment and weaponry used are never discussed. Beevor keeps most of his attention on the battlefield which may disappoint readers looking for a more comprehensive treatment of a global military/political/economic event, and his chapter on Yalta is an excellent example of how good this book could have been had he chosen to broaden his focus a bit. Other decisions, such as devoting pages to Hitler's final days in his bunker but only a few paragraphs to Hiroshima/Nagasaki are questionable.<br /><br />I think this book is on par with Penguin's History of the Second World War. Penguin's volume has better maps, a broader focus, and does a much better job of addressing the causes and consequences of the war. It is also much longer though and separates the European and Pacific theaters into separate parts. I strongly recommend Rick Atkinson's series for readers wanting a battlefield narrative from an American perspective.

B008JMKN4Y|5.0|I devoured this book with an appetite so far beyond famished that I grin sheepishly as I FINALLY review one of the best books I have read in a very VERY long time. *contented sigh*<br /><br />It's hard to let go of truly impactful characters who take you through a plethora of emotion. I felt somewhat discombobulated, distraught, electrified, and madly in the deepest bowels of affection for those involved as I read Travis and Abby`s story. I was, by all accounts, right there with these two uniquely beautiful characters as they navigated their way through heartache, pain, betrayal, identity stereotypes, friendship, and yes, finally love.<br /><br />The secondary characters lend validity to our Hero and heroine's plight as they come to terms with not only who they identify themselves as being but also what they represent together as a unified front. America and Shepley offer insightful secondhand commentary as part of \\"the circle\\" that is at times surreal and thought provoking. I know I'm rambling but I digress...<br /><br />Onward with the review:<br /><br />So what is all the hype about you ask? Well, for starters, there's Travis 'Mad Dog' Maddox. Bad boy extraordinaire. He's tattooed, drives a motorcycle, has repugnant taste in non-committal one night stands, has a cousin, Shepley, who shares an apartment with him (and also provides much entertainment as the `voice of reason` at times), drinks way too much than is otherwise healthy, and oh yeah fights hand to hand boxing/combat style at Eastern University's super secret underground \\"Circle\\". A bet is initiated and accepted and convoluted misinterpretations ensue. Without spoiling the story for those of you who haven`t read it, and without writing too much of the plot as is warranted, I leave you with this. Buy. The. Book. Go to the library and borrow it if you have to. But read it already! I ensure you that you won't be sorry.<br /><br />In a day and age where college and sex are synonymous Jamie Mcguire brings us a story that encompasses so much more than the epitomized stigma that college students face today. It's been one of the wildest rides in literary fiction I have ever had the pleasure of discovering.<br /><br />I give this story:<br /><br />5 stars for awesomeness<br />5 stars for originality<br />4 stars for punctuation, grammar, etc. (blah blah blah)<br />3.5 stars for book front cover art ( I get the meaning behind the new cover but I liked the eclecticity of the original. B008JGSM6G|4.0|I enjoy Flow Free tremendously.  I have Router (and Aqua Router), and have played them for many months.  So I wasn't sure what Flow Free would bring to the table that would be new.  Some have even mentioned the similarity.  However, there are (at least) two things that are different about Flow Free that make it as challenging -- or more so -- than Router.  In Router, you just need to complete the paths connecting the colored dots.  In Flow Free, you must do that -- but do it by using every square in the grid!  And, as a side point, do it in the least possible number of moves -- or you don't get a \\"Perfect\\" score.  Many times, in the harder puzzles (8x8 and larger), I'd connect all of the dots successfully -- but there were still open squares in the grid, and I needed to rethink my logic.<br /><br />The second thing is that Flow Free has a greater range.  In Router, the board is always the same size.  Sometimes there are squares that are \\"missing\\" -- i.e., you can't play on those squares; but that's the extent of the difference.  In Flow Free, you can play the (really trivial) 5x5 square puzzles, or move to 6x6, or 7x7.  Then as you master those (and it won't take long), that's when it starts to become challenging.  The 8x8 grids slowed me down; but then there are 9x9, 10x10, 11x11, 12x12, 13x13, and even 14x14 grids!  Now imagine that you not only have to connect 30 or 40 colored dots (without crossing lines); but you have to do it without leaving any blank squares!  The higher levels are tagged \\"Impossible\\", and they really are.  So you'll never run out of things to do.<br /><br />I should add that there's yet another difference to Router and other similar games -- you can play in Puzzle Mode (like the others -- you have all the time you need); or you can play in Timed Mode -- in which case, you have a fixed amount of time (that you select from half a dozen pre-determined time quantities), and the object is to see how quickly you can solve one puzzle after another in that time.<br /><br />I had no problems at all with see all of the grids on my Motorola Xoom (though I wouldn't want to try the 14x14 grids on a 3\\" phone screen!)  Nor did I have any problems with manipulating the grids, drawing the lines, etc.  I suspect that some of the 1-star ratings that had problems were possibly on lower-resolution, or smaller-screen, devices. B0070UFMOW|4.0|The comments from those who cannot perceive a difference between an amped and unamped source should be discounted on the spot, there, I said it.  Ok, now onto the review.    The fiio E17, albeit not the prime package for this price range, does in fact improve the quality of your sound, but depending on your cans, you may not hear it.  So the first step is to get a good pair of headphones.  I use these to run my HD25's, JH5pros, D2000, ER4-S, and others, and I can hear a difference across the board.    It starts with noise. By virtue of using an amp/DAC, you are bypassing the crappy D/A on your computer, which, in my case, is a MacBook Pro.  To the guy who said that macbook pro's use good D/A's, I suggest you get your ears checked.  The noise from my 2010 13' is horrendous, and any one of my sensitive pairs of cans can pick up on it.  Noise is an extreme detriment to clarity, and by using the Fiio's D/A function, you use it's industry-leading wolfson chip instead (much cleaner signal). As a result, the noise is extremely low, and you have a very dark background to enjoy your music with.    Another reason you want an amp is so your music maintains it's Pace, Rhythm, and Timing (PRaT), to the best extent possible. Cans and D/A's with bad PRaT detract from the music experience, and oftentimes make the music sound congested and less energetic.    Clarity is another reason why you use an DAC/amp combo. Separation with the FiioE17 is way beyond anything that you can get unamped. Bass is tightened up, midrange is presented with more authority, and the highs are clear and crisp. The fact is that most sources (i.e. your computer or ipod) do not keep the ideal power requirements for music, and again, the fiioE17 fixes that, improving the immediacy of each sound wave.    In terms of the Fiio's sound signature, it's slightly brighter than an unamped source, mids are drier, and the bass is tightened considerably. Separation and spatial positioning are increased in the music, andon the whole, everything retains more detail. Again this is not to say that this is the end game for portable DAC/amp combos, because it surely is not.    I really appreciate the on board EQ feature, which allows me to tweak bass levels five levels up or down, and also treble levels the same way. However, be wary of turning up the bass, it can muddy up the sound and doesn't give music any more thump, as the upper range is more responsible for impact. This is still useful in fixing random FR peaks and valleys from your source or headphone. In addition to that, the Fiio also features a Coax and optical input option (very cool!), which, I think can both be used to play at 192/24 rates. For the conventional usb DAC, you can play up to 96/24 bit. Its pure amp function can be used via the aux port, which you can plug a LOD from your ipod for a better signal. Upsampling is usually unnecessary, but at least it can help with aliasing (hotly contested topic, I know). IF I remember correctly, it can run up to 250 ohm impedances, so look elsewhere for a more powerful amp.    The form factor is on the thick side, but can still be carried in the pocket as part of a portable setup.    Overall, I have to say it does what DAC/amps are supposed to do, and has a plethora of features to accompany it. It reduces the noise of your music, and will improves fidelity and spatial resolution. I do not have enough experience to say how it compares to others, but I can say it does the job it's supposed to.    Edit: minor clarification.    2 year followup:  This proves to be a great dj'ing soundcard and pre-amp! Very unorthodox, I know. It doesn't feature multiple outputs, but I use my computer audio port for pre-listening. Works like a charm.  The wolfson chip in the E17 is only featured in flagship pioneer and rane systems, which run upwards of $1500, so having $140 kit with the same specs is awesome.  I EQ and compress the bass in my music and this amp can solidly pump out every additional decibel of it. Who knew audiophile equipment could be used in the field!

No questions asked, Rubinstein's playing is flawless, and his accompaniment is carefully measured under Reiner's baton.  Together, piano and orchestra produce wonderful music, full of excitement, flowing melodies that breath without lingering into sentimentality, and in terrific quality period sound.<br /><br />The demonic Rhapsody on a Theme by Pagaini is superb, in every way.  Rubinstein articulates each note in this notoriously challenging set of variations.  The piano sound is deep, crisp, very present. The sound is wonderful, and the chemistry between two giants of their time, Arthur Rubinstein and Fritz Reiner with his CSO, has the electricity of a live performance. The recording is historic just for those reasons, but the added bonus of the product being so enjoyable, so truthful to Rachmaninoff's scores, so invigorating, really self-recommends this item. 0307352145|5.0|When I first became aware of Quiet, I knew it was a book I had to read.  I've always been an introvert, content to curl up with a book for hours and wary of crowded places and loud parties.  Yet, even though I was aware of my introversion, I don't think I really understood it and what it meant.  I always saw it as a sort of flexible personality trait that I could change if I wanted to, and I always had the sense that there was something unhealthy about my particular personality type.  After reading the book, I feel I have a much better understanding not only of myself, but of how the way American society is structured tends to work against someone like me.<br /><br />Cain uses a blend of some of the latest neurological research and years of psychiatric studies to show why introverts and extroverts are so different, and to lay rest to the idea that there's something broken in people who don't meet the gregarious, outgoing American ideal.  The neuroscience she highlights in the book is easily understandable to the layperson, and shows how the brains of introverts and extroverts process stimuli differently.  Quite literally, extroverts' brains function in such a way that they are exhilarated and recharged by plenty of social stimulation while introverts need quiet and downtime in order to recharge.<br /><br />Using this research, Cain then shows why so many aspects of modern American life are doing few favors for introverts and, in fact, may be hindering creativity and innovation overall.  One of the topics she discusses is the seemingly ubiquitous open-plan workspace.  Having spent some years in offices, I could feel myself recoil as soon as she began touching on the topic.  The idea behind these plans is that an open atmosphere will create more interaction between employees, thus stimulating creativity and helping to further develop ideas.  This goes hand-in-hand with the corporate love of brainstorming sessions and leadership retreats, all of which I've had the misfortune of attending.  However, as Cain shows, all of these things actually hinder creativity.  People are at their most creative when they have quiet time alone in which to let their mind roam freely.  This is a topic of particular interest to me, and there are plenty of sources that support this conclusion.  Some companies are beginning to recognize this problem and are starting to design offices that provide spaces where people can retreat for some quiet thought and reflection.  This is a boon not only to the introverts, who really need these spaces, but to the extroverts as well because these more thoughtfully designed spaces offer all personality types the opportunity to seek out stimulation when they need it and to retreat into privacy when they need time for quiet thought.<br /><br />Another point that I found particularly interesting was Cain's examination of current educational philosophies.  I'm a former high/middle school teacher and the parent of an elementary-aged child, so I've had the chance to see this in action--not to mention the styles and philosophies with which I was presented during my teacher training.  There currently is a HUGE emphasis on group work, which I think can be attributed to the corporatization of education.  Despite that I am, myself, an introvert, as a teacher, I bought into the idea that kids need to be constantly interacting, but Cain points out the flaws in this idea.  Both my colleagues and myself were sometimes guilty of the \\"Johnny is a great student but so quiet--if only he'd talk more!\\" syndrome, and this book made me realize how harmful that can actually be.  While there is emphasis in modern education on learning styles--the visual learner versus the auditory learner, for example--there is next to no emphasis on how to appeal to various personality types.  I volunteer in my daughter's school, and every year the kids' desks are arranged in groupings, so that students spend a large amount of their time working with other students.  Still, there is a bright spot in that they are occasionally granted small stretches of time during which they're free to retreat to whatever part of their classroom or the outside pod area they like so they may complete an assignment.  Quiet really made me think about how important these opportunities are, and how they need to become a more integral part of the educational system.<br /><br />Most valuable of all, though, this book really made me think deeply about myself and those I love and to think of ways to better interact with them.  I have a better understand now of how what seemed pliable personality traits are actually inherent in me, and how I can work with them instead of against them so that I can alleviate stress and avoid feeling overwhelmed and burned out.  While the book is primarily about introverts, it also helped me to gain a better understanding about the extroverts in my life and how we can live more harmoniously with one another.  The sections of the book that deal with married couples was particularly interesting in this respect, because they showed how our own biases make us judge the reactions of others.  Since we tend to see the world through a certain lens, we sometimes have trouble understanding that not everyone thinks the way we do, and that we may be taking their reactions and misunderstanding them.  This is truly invaluable because I realize that by taking a more thoughtful approach to interactions with others, I can better understand not only my actions but theirs, which will hopefully lead to a better level of understanding between us.<br /><br />Quiet is a book I would recommend to anyone, regardless of whether you're an introvert or an extrovert.  I think that introverts like myself will feel better about our personalities and will see that the way we operate can be a strength rather than a social liability, as many of us have been conditioned to believe.  And I think that extroverts can learn that introverts aren't necessarily socially backwards or cold, but people who often feel awkward in our own skins because we see and admire the outgoing traits of extroverts but may have trouble emulating them.  This book simply works on so many levels: from the corporate world to the world of education and right on down to a personal level.

Briefly, and without revealing too much, since the pleasure of the novel is revelation upon revelation, twist after twist, Nick and Amy meet in New York, fall in love, marry, and seem happy, until both lose their jobs.  Nick decides to move back to his hometown of North Carthage, MO, ostensibly to help care for his dying mother and Alzheimer father.  Then Amy disappears, apparently abducted by parties unknown.  It isn't long before the police settle the mantle of prime suspect on Nick's shoulders.  And the question is who took Amy and why, or did hubby murder her?  But beware:  nothing is as it seems.<br /><br />Flynn tells the story from Nick and Amy's perspectives, leaving us to wonder, at least in the first part of the book, who we are to believe.  In part two, Flynn reveals what really happened and why.  In part three, she resolves the situation.  Some have faulted Flynn on the ending, but I found it perfectly acceptable, in keeping with the personalities of the characters.  However, morally tidy it is not.<br /><br />Flynn skillfully handles sociopathy, having her sociopathic character expound, as well as showing how others perceive or misperceive the sociopath.  It's claimed that somewhere around four percent of the U.S. population is sociopathic.  Not all, or even a fraction of, sociopaths are psychopaths, and they are nearly impossible, by their very nature, to spot.  One trait, however, can give them away; that is, the compulsion to escalate their behavior, something Flynn expertly illustrates.<br /><br />If psychopathic killers are your thing, see my list of top-notch titles in my Listmania \\"Killer Crime Fiction,\\" by clicking Explorer.

Jennifer Lawrence embodies the lead like no other, her strength, her courage and her ability to overcome obstacles is portrayed perfectly on screen, I couldn't image a better actress in the role. Likewise, the story was beautifully written and well told. For those unfamiliar, The Hunger Games is about a futuristic totalitarian society that split North America into twelve districts after an unknown war, since then two tributes from each district are offered up and forced to combat on a sick reality-type show, thats a lot of story to convey onscreen. Thankfully Ross was up for the challenge and alongside author Suzanne Collins, the two managed to bust out a story that was faithful to its series, while still keeping its distance and originality. Its also managed to keep the story loyal without becoming a slave to its love triangle, this isn't vampires and werewolves meets Panem. While there are elements of romance in the story, like Collins intended, the film focuses mainly on the oppression of society and its cruelty to those in it. The film is without a doubt a recommendation, and a well deserved portrayal of its book predecessor. The only problem now is Ross is no longer in control of the series, the baton now passes to director Francis Lawrence for the next one, and whoever else is available for the last. I sincerely hope Lawrence can follow Ross's example and bring another great installment to the series, in a world filled with reboots, prequels and unnecessary sequels, it'd be nice to have a great trilogy from the start.

1451648537|3.0|\\"Some consider UNIX to be the second most important invention to come out of AT&T Bell Labs after the transistor.\\"<br />-- Dennis Ritchie<br /><br />\\"Those who do not understand UNIX are condemned to reinvent it, poorly.\\"<br />-- Henry Spencer<br /><br />With nearly fifteen hundred customers providing feedback on the virtues and shortcomings of Walter Isaacson's biography of Steve Jobs, I would ordinarily refrain from comment.  But I believe Isaacson has misrepresented the development of computing over the past forty years in a way that previous reviewers have not commented on, and as someone who had occasion to view these events from several perspectives, I would like to apply some corrective spin.<br /><br />In several places in his book, Isaacson posits a dichotomy between \\"open\\" and \\"closed\\" systems (e.g., pp. 554 and 561-62 in the hardcopy edition).  Isaacson is not using these terms as they are conventionally used today; rather, he is referring to the degree of integration between the software and hardware of a product.  In today's terminology, an \\"open\\" system is a technology whose implementation details are available for review and modification by anyone who cares to do so.  Anyone who has followed the evolution of computing systems over the past few decades will be astonished to hear that Bill Gates and Microsoft are deemed by Isaacson to be emblematic of the \\"open\\" approach, while Steve Jobs and Apple are deemed the founders of \\"the other camp\\" (i.e., the \\"closed\\" approach).  This would be silly if it weren't so misleading.<br /><br />Gore Vidal wrote in 1977 that \\"There is only one party in the United States, the Property Party ... and it has two right wings, Republican and Democrat.\\"  Throughout most of their existence, both Microsoft and Apple have been solidly at the \\"closed\\" end of the spectrum.  The difference has been one of degree and and results:  Microsoft, backed by the IBM giant, shipped products infamous for being buggy, difficult to use, andnotoriously difficult to integrate with other systems.  By contrast, Apple shipped products that were elegantly designed, very easy to use, but often of limited functionality, and often difficult to integrate with non-Apple environments.<br /><br />The elephant in the room, of course, is UNIX, developed at Bell Laboratories in the 1970s by some of the best software engineers of the century, and still thriving in numerous incarnations (including Mac OS X) today.  I read [[ASIN:1451648537 Steve Jobs]] immediately after reading John Gertner's [[ASIN:1594203288 The Idea Factory: Bell Labs and the Great Age of American Innovation]].  Bell Laboratories had fostered an environment of innovation decades before Steve Jobs strove to create a similar environment at Apple.  UNIX had sophisticated desktop publishing by the mid-1970s -- it simply wasn't commercially available.  UNIX networking code developed at the University of California-Berkeley laid the foundation for the implementation of the Internet as we know it today.  Isaacson seems oblivious to all of this in his search for a facile comparison of Microsoft and Apple.<br /><br />Up until the advent of Mac OS X, the metaphor I used the compare the various systems was something like this:<br /><br />Macintosh: a two-seater sports car -- lovely to look at, fun to drive, but you couldn't haul a trailer with it.<br /><br />Windows: a 1930s box truck built in Mexico from stolen and makeshift parts held together with duct tape and bailing wire, with a broken muffler, spewing black smoke, easy to hotwire, no cargo lift, and prone to stalling out frequently for mysterious reasons.<br /><br />UNIX: a system with the power of a freight train, the speed and maneuverability of a fighter jet, and a commensurate learning curve.<br /><br />Gates is disingenuous (and Isaacson is too technically illiterate to contradict him) when he says that Microsoft is for \\"standards\\" and that Apple's products have built-in \\"incompatibility\\" (p. 230).Microsoft is notorious for its incompatible implementations of standards ... for its secret interfaces ... and for its hideously complex and Byzantine implementation that makes security analysis impossible.  Steve Johnson (a major contributor to early UNIX development) once quipped that \\"Using TSO [an interactive IBM environment] is like kicking a dead whale down the beach.\\"  The same could be said of using DOS/Windows.  (My own metaphor is that doing anything with Windows is like trying to build a ship in a bottle using robot arms.)  Microsoft made money and won market share by shipping shoddy products that would never have succeeded on their own merits without having become a \\"standard\\" through IBM's original partnership.  In 2005, security consultant Dan Geer estimated that \\"over half of the productivity gains computers should have brought the domestic economy were lost due to standardization on the Redmond platform\\" (;login, vol. 30, no. 6, December 2005).  Isaacson quotes Gates as deriding the NeXT technology as \\"warmed-over UNIX\\" (p.  302) -- what Gates never understood is that even warmed-over UNIX is orders of magnitude better than any product Microsoft ever shipped.<br /><br />Since my early thirties, I have had the good fortune to work in a number of UNIX environments.  In 1978, I started doing serious academic publishing using the version of UNIX developed at the University of California-Berkeley, running on DEC PDP-11/70s and later a VAX-11/780.  In 1985, I joined AIDA, a Santa Clara start-up company developing software for designing VLSI chips (the software was later acquired by Fujitsu and used to develop the first 64-bit SPARC chip), and worked on Apollo workstations running Domain/OS (Apollo's implementation of UNIX).  In 1988, I joined Sun Microsystems in Mountain View, using SunOS (and later Solaris) on Sun workstations.  I worked for Sun for seventeen years as a technical writer, manager, and tools programmer.<br /><br />I was laid off by Sun in2005, and found myself with a problem:  I owned no personal computer with which to pursue job hunting or personal projects -- over the decades, all of my work had been done on my employers' equipment, either directly or through dial-up connections using simple ASCII terminals.  (The only personal computer I had ever owned was a Kaypro II running CP/M, purchased in 1983 and retired a few years later when it was dropped in transit.) At first I tried using public computers at the local library, but the invariable Windows machines combined with a two-hour usage limit quickly demonstrated that this would be impractical.<br /><br />Moreover, my expectations had changed.  A friend had given me an iPod Shuffle, and I wanted to transfer music to it from my extensive CD collection.  I had become interested in digitizing family photographs and sharing these through email attachments or optical media.  And I had friends around the country with whom I wanted to video conference using Skype.  In short, it wasn't just about text any more, and I wanted to join the twenty-first century.<br /><br />I briefly considered purchasing a Windows box, tempted by the mirage of \\"standards\\" and \\"interoperability\\", but I quickly rejected this -- I loathed Microsoft and its products, and my experiences attempting to integrate peripherals with friends' Windows computers had confirmed the ongoing difficulty of working with these machines.  Moreover, they lacked even a semblance of the UNIX utilities upon which all of my work was based.  No Windows.<br /><br />At the other end of the scale would have been something running Linux.  But despite my experience with UNIX, Linux was relatively unknown to me.  Moreover, while I applauded the open source movement, Linux seemed a chaotic and confusing world, with infinite choices and possibilities, but also infinite complications.  I had little time to spend coming up to speed on Linux and the various hardware possibilities and the complexities of configuring a working system.  I wanted a machine that someone else had integrated and configured.<br /><br />I have a good friend who has worked with Apple computers for most of her career.  She owned an iBook running MAC OS X \\"Tiger\\", and after examining the machine, I decided to buy a Mac.  MAC OS X has a respectable hybrid lineage, the love child of the Mach kernel from Carnegie-Mellon and various offspring of BSD UNIX.  Late in 2007, I purchased an iMac with a 500 GB disk and a 17\\" screen.  It had a built-in camera and a built-in microphone.  I unpacked it (admiring the quality and artistry of the packaging), plugged it in, and installed the operating system from the CDs.  It just worked.<br /><br />Over the months, I discovered that it had more than a camera and microphone.  The built-in optical drive could both read and burn CDs and DVDs.  Bundled with the system were languages like Perl, Python, and PHP.  All of the standard UNIX utilities were provided, which for a programmer is like having a machine shop and a complete set of carpenters' tools at hand.  There is an SQL client named SQLite that feels remarkably like early versions of the Ingres relational database.  On the XCode CD are not just useful utilities such as RCS and make, but a full implementation of the GNU compiler and development suite (which meant that I could download and compile source code for thousands of open source programs if I chose to do so).  There was groff, the GNU implementation of troff, a full-bore typesetting environment which I make extensive use of.  I have yet to discover the full extent of what has been bundled with the system.<br /><br />Not everything I wanted was bundled with the system, and over the succeeding months, I downloaded and installed the \\"R\\" statistical language ... OpenOffice and LibreOffice (open source implementations of the Microsoft Office suite of applications) ... Gimp (an open source image processing application analogous to Adobe Photoshop) ... Audacity (anopen source application for editing sound and music files) ...  the Firefox browser ... and much more.  I downloaded pre-compiled binaries, not wanting to spend time wrestling with compiling and recompiling.  They just worked.<br /><br />Now that's what I call an open product, Mr. Isaacson!<br /><br />Moreover, I really enjoy shopping at Apple Stores.  They are clean, attractive, well-lit, and well-staffed with intelligent, friendly, helpful people who are considerate and respectful of the fact that my time is valuable.  Any staff member can process your purchase, and since I usually know exactly what I want, I'm usually in and out of the store in less than ten minutes.  This is a stark contrast, say, to Fry's Electronics stores in the San Francisco Bay Area: dingy, poorly-lit warehouses staffed by indifferent (and sometimes incompetent) sales people, with long, meandering checkout lines and scowling security staff prominently placed at the exits.<br /><br />In 1991, when I managed the Solaris documentation group at Sun for some months, my team (many of whom greatly admired Apple products) speculated about a \\"dream machine,\\" a workstation with a UNIX engine and the Apple GUI.  In 1996, when Apple's CEO tried to convince Sun to purchase Apple, our hopes were again roused, only to be dashed when Sun rejected the overture.<br /><br />In the past five years, I have purchased four of these \\"dream machines\\" (my original iMac and three MacBook Pros).  They are made by Apple.

But before Living Things, before A Thousand Suns, before Minutes to Midnight, before Meteora, there was Hybrid Theory.<br /><br />To give you a little history, Linkin Park originated in 1996 under the name Xero. The band members of this band were Rob Bourdon--a percussionist; Mike Shinoda--a vocalist, rhythm guitar player, keyboard player, and pianist; Brad Delson--a guitar and keyboard player; Dave \\"Phoenix\\" Farrel--bass player; and Mark Wakefield--the main vocalist. It wasn't until 1998 when Wakefield left the band behind and allowed the current vocalist, Chester Bennington, to join the group and show off his amazing singing talent.<br /><br />After proving himself worthy of the band's attention, Chester Bennington became one of the few people to sing like an \\"angel\\" and scream like a \\"demon.\\" It was shortly after Bennington's arrival when Xero decided to rename themselves Hybrid Theory: a theory to create a successful hybrid of musical genres. Shortly thereafter did the band finally decide to call themselves Linkin Park--after receiving some inspiration from Santa Monica's Lincoln Park.<br /><br />Finally in 1999 did Linkin Park sign a deal with Warner Bros. studios to release their new album: Hybrid Theory. In it are 12 songs: Papercut, One Step Closer, With You, Points of Authority, Crawling, Runaway, By Myself, A Place for My Head, Forgotten, Cure for The Itch, and Pushing Me Away.<br /><br />Each of the songs hold their own kind of musical genre to them. We get to hear an equal balance between rap, hard rock, screamo; the list goes on. But that's what's so special about Hybrid Theory--and Linkin Park's music in general. You can listen to their music and you won't think any of the genres listed above; instead you'll think \\"this is Linkin Park.\\"<br /><br />Throughout the course of Hybrid Theory's songs do we alternate between Shinoda's rapping, Bennington's screams, and Brad's guitar. All the while do we get a deep sense of emotion for the six men performing for us. Such emotions contain anger, sorrow, and rebellion--three emotions I yearn for when I'm writing my books. Maybe it's just me, but these emotions really appeal to me.<br /><br />In a world where music is dominated by autotuning and electronic noises, I think I speak for all the rock and roll fans when I say more people need to make albums like this. Say what you will about Linkin Park, there's no denying Hybrid Theory is just plain awesomeness.t that's what's so special about Hybrid Theory--and Linkin Park's music in general. You can listen to their music and you won't think any of the genres listed above; instead you'll think \\"this is Linkin Park.\\"<br /><br />Throughout the course of Hybrid Theory's songs do we alternate between Shinoda's rapping, Bennington's screams, and Brad's guitar. 0385523912|5.0|Having known very little about North or South Korea beyond what I'd read recently (about a prison camp escapee and the experiences of two  American journalists imprisoned for trespassing) prior to reading this book, I found these oral histories absolutely fascinating, so much so that I listened to the audio version (read by Karen White, whose kindergarten teacher tone didn't work for me on such serious topics) and then experienced it a second time by reading the book myself.<br /><br />The stories that the defectors shared with Ms. Demick are similar in many ways. These persons grew up with a strong sense of patriotism, solid in their beliefs that what they'd been told about their country and (and by) The Dear Leader was the gospel. B002BSHXJA|5.0|Like many other reviewers, I'm a huge Beatles fan -- I've read numerous books about them from all kinds of different perspectives, I've watched just about every documentary ever released, read every interview and article I could get my hands on, and, of course, listened to their entire catalog of songs many times over. I can honestly say, with all the time I've spent celebrating the Beatles, taking into account what they've meant to me from my childhood, how they shaped me as a musician, and how my worldview has been affected by their songs, I had no idea what I was missing with the mono recordings.<br /><br />I am a child of the late 70s, which means I grew up listening to the Beatles secondhand, on scratchy vinyl records, warped cassette tapes, and of course, the legendarily bad late-1980s CD releases. As good as the music is, I don't think I truly heard it as the Beatles intended until I received the Mono Box Set as a gift last Christmas. When the Remasters came out in 2009, like everyone else, I made a choice and went with the Stereo set. That set is excellent, of course, and I recommend that any true Beatles fan should own both the Mono and Stereo sets. But having listened to both, I assert that the Mono Set, with few exceptions, is the best sonic Beatles experience that you'll ever have.<br /><br />The biggest, shining example of that is Sgt. Peppers. The album comes alive in Mono like I've never heard before. I have heard that John Lennon once said that the only way to listen to Sgt. Pepper is in mono, and boy was he right. The track listing is the same as the Stereo counterpart, but the sound and feel of the Mono version is extremely different. \\"She's Leaving Home,\\" for instance, is in a completely different key in Mono because, as the Beatles intended, the tape was sped up, giving it a vibrant feel that the Stereo version lacks. \\"Lucy in the Sky with Diamonds,\\" a song I grew tired of once I got older, is now almost new to me in Mono, and reminds me of why the song is so good. In Mono, John's voice and the instruments are given a flange effect which is completely absent from the Stereo version. How the engineers and the guys who mixed the tracks got away with leaving those elements out of the Stereo mix is beyond me. Other tracks, like the title song, and \\"Good Morning, Good Morning\\" have more muscle and feel more rock-and-roll than before. \\"Fixing a Hole\\" sounds especially good now, with the enchanting harpsichord remaining more audible throughout the song, and you can also hear Paul's vocals during the fadeout in a different way, including some soulful scat-singing as the song fades away. None of that is present in stereo, and it is a true shame.<br /><br />The other albums also benefit from the power of Mono -- you get all of the sound coming from the center instead of being split left and right, which puts the music right in your face. Some examples of Mono's rock-and-roll feel can be heard in the tracks \\"The Night Before\\" from Help!, where the intro sounds punchier and starts off a groove that is absent from Stereo, and \\"Ticket to Ride\\" from the same album, with its iconic intro guitar lick, sounds better than ever. \\"Hey Jude,\\" from the especially enjoyable \\"Mono Masters\\" compilation, has more power. I could go on and on, song-by-song, but I think you get the idea. Overall, the Stereo versions, by and large, now seem like a watered-down, weakened version that won't ever make it back into my listening rotation.<br /><br />That's not to say, however, that the Mono mixes don't have *some* weaknesses. I agree with other reviewers on here that the song \\"Help!\\" sounds especially muddy in Mono, and it is one of the few tracks that I prefer in Stereo. And you don't get \\"Abbey Road\\" or \\"Let it Be\\" at all in this collection since no Mono mix was ever made for those albums -- understandable, especially with \\"Abbey Road,\\" since the recording techniques were more sophisticated at that point. The weaknesses are minor, however,and on balance, the Mono set easily trumps its counterpart.<br /><br />A note on the packaging -- the albums are presented as \\"mini-LPs,\\" complete with the original dust sleeves and album artwork, photos, and inserts. Remember the goofy cut-outs from Sgt. Pepper? There here, same as in 1967 when the album was first released. Especially nice is the photo booklet from \\"Magical Mystery Tour,\\" and the mini-poster from \\"The Beatles (White Album).\\" You also get a nice case to store the discs in. It's different than the Stereo set, which is nice.<br /><br />(If you rip the songs to your computer/MP3 player, I recommend using the program Exact Audio Copy, or something similar, to make sure the integrity of each track was intact without compression. If you spend good money to get pristine remastered tracks, don't abuse the recordings by compressing them to MP3s!)<br /><br />If you've been thinking about buying this set but wondering if it's worth it, I can guarantee you it is. Stop hesitating and grab it now.

I was laughing, especially in the early part of the movie. There's just something naturally funny about real young kids or their comic depictions using really course gutter language. I guess it's because it's so unexpected. The kids are so young they couldn't possibly know what they're saying and there it is right out in the open for anyone to hear. My mother would have threatened to wash my mouth out with soap if she ever heard even a small fraction of such utterings. And my childhood gutter vocabulary was nowhere near that of the South Park characters. Plus, there's a lot of breaking wind, throwing up, and eliminating bodily wastes. Why would this Junior-High-level humor be so funny? I guess it's because it's so impolite and the audience feels embarrassed. Laughter is a relief, after all. And I was laughing, right along with everyone else, although I suspect my laughter was nowhere nearly as vigorous as is indicated by others in their reviews. Some of the reviews suggested that the movie was funniest in the first part and then tapered off. That's an interesting observation. This type of verbal onslaught is funniest when you first hear it, but the effect wears off as it continues. That was my experience. After a while it was just no longer very funny and I began wondering when the movie would be over.<br /><br />The story was irrelevant and just a vehicle for the comic language and coarse visual gags. No one would watch the story or find it amusing were the coarse language not there. Also, South Park animated cartoons are primitive, to say the least. They're somewhat clever and amusing, but other animations are far superior. Okay, I was laughing and enjoyed the movie. Would I watch it again? Not likely.<br /><br />Gary Petersonc language and coarse visual gags. No one would watch the story or find it amusing were the coarse language not there. Also, South Park animated cartoons are primitive, to say the least. They're somewhat clever and amusing, but other animations are far superior. Okay, I was laughing and enjoyed the movie. Would I watch it again? Not likely.<br /><br />Gary Peterson
B000002U9W|3.0|\\"Atom Heart Mother\\" does not rank high in the Pink Floyd canon. It lacks the focus of their later work, including the melodic punch of \\"Dark Side of the Moon\\" and the rangier but still controlled pulse of \\"Wish You Were Here.\\"<br /><br />Yet it's an important album, because this is the record on which the Floyd began to find its footing after losing one of its founding members, guitarist/vocalist/songwriter Syd Barrett, to the ravages of mental illness that was possibly exacerbated by drug use.<br /><br />After the early glory of the Barrett-led \\"The Piper at the Gates of Dawn,\\" PF had released \\"A Saucerful of Secrets,\\" \\"More\\" and \\"Ummagumma.\\" Each of those albums had its distinct problems -- for more information, see my reviews of each. It wasn't until \\"Atom Heart\\" that the post-Barrett Pink Floyd began to sound sure of its direction. \\"Atom Heart,\\" in fact, provided a blueprint the Floyd would follow on almost every subsequent album; it offered a long piece, a somewhat long piece, and several shorter, more \\"personal\\" compositions.<br /><br />The title track, in the original vinyl configuration, took up the entirety of Side One. It's an ambitious piece of music, with the four members of the band being augmented by an orchestra and choir. In places, it sounds absolutely majestic -- there's definitely a classical feel here, but you can also tell it's a rock band providing the pulse. Unfortunately, it has significant timing problems. Bassist Roger Waters and drummer Nick Mason slow down and speed up, and guitarist David Gilmour and keyboardist Rick Wright basically follow them over the various tempo cliffs.<br /><br />Side Two offers some interesting songs. Waters' \\"If\\" is a lovely ballad, musically speaking, although the lyrics are much harder-edged, with references to insanity that would resurface and intensify by the time he would begin working on the lyrics for \\"Dark Side of the Moon.\\" Rick Wright's \\"Summer '68\\" is definitely of its time; you can practically smell the cannabis and incense, but it's a tuneful, enjoyable piece of pop craftsmanship. David Gilmour's \\"Fat Old Sun\\" begins very gently but builds as Gilmour starts layering in the electric guitar parts. And the album concludes with the group composition \\"Alan's Psychedelic Breakfast,\\" which I loved when I was a teen-ager but find rather annoying now. The voice-overs that would seem so pithy and poignant on \\"Dark Side\\" are, here, just grating -- I really don't need to hear some English chap musing about how much he likes marmalade, or how his back hurts him when he works.<br /><br />This isn't an album I listen to very much anymore, but Floyd fans without much knowledge of the band's distant past might enjoy hearing it. And, again, it's an important transitional work, paving the way for \\"Meddle,\\" on which the Floyd would perfect many of the concepts here.the cannabis and incense, but it's a tuneful, enjoyable piece of pop craftsmanship. B004ZN9J0K|3.0|\\"A Saucerful of Secrets\\" is vastly overrated by many Pink Floyd fans. The truth is that the band's second album was marred by a paucity of fresh songwriting. This wasn't a surprise to followers of the group at the time; the Floyd had lost co-founder and guitarist/vocalist/songwriter Syd Barrett to an ugly, debilitating collision of mental illness and excessive drug use. He was replaced by David Gilmour, an equally capable vocalist and, actually, a better guitar player -- but Gilmour, at the time, lacked Barrett's imaginative songwriting skills. Bassist Roger Waters and keyboardist Rick Wright were striving to contribute; drummer Nick Mason has never been a strong songwriting force in the band.<br /><br />In short, Barrett had written most of the Floyd's material, and without him, the rest of the band seemed kind of lost. (One Barrett track, \\"Jugband Blues,\\" does appear on \\"Saucerful,\\" but it's not Barrett's best work.) Of the seven tracks on \\"Saucerful,\\" Waters writes three on his own -- \\"Let There Be More Light,\\" \\"Corporal Clegg\\" and \\"Set the Controls for the Heart of the Sun,\\" and only the latter track makes a strong impression, with its menacing, rumbling tone. Wright's \\"Remember a Day\\" and \\"See-Saw\\" are pleasant enough, musically speaking, but, ultimately, forgettable. The group-composed title track is a longer, more ambitious piece, but it just doesn't seem to go anywhere. The best Floyd music always has been a kind of aural journey, and this piece simply doesn't know what it wants to be.<br /><br />Still, \\"Saucerful\\" has some nice moments, and was proof the Floyd could soldier on without Barrett. And it wouldn't be long before the glory days of \\"Dark Side of the Moon\\" and \\"Wish You Were Here.\\"

B004ZN9NCY|4.0|\\"Ummagumma\\" was, for a long time, a personal favorite of mine; as I've aged, the album has sounded less and less relevant to me.<br /><br />It originally consisted of two vinyl discs. The second disc featured four segments in which each band member was to do pretty much whatever he wanted.<br /><br />Of the four, only Roger Waters seems to have developed enough as a writer at the time to pull this off -- his \\"Grantchester Meadows\\" was a languid but compelling aural home movie, which flowed into one of the strangest tracks ever on a Floyd album: \\"Several Species of Small Furry Animals Gathered Together in a Cave and Grooving With a Pict.\\" Keyboardist Rick Wright's \\"Sysyphus\\" was a 12-minute opus featuring lots of rumbling, low pianos and strange tones; it was frequently quite compelling and listenable, but droned on a bit too long. David Gilmour's \\"The Narrow Way\\" ... well, Gilmour himself later admitted he was just muddling along here (I'd like to quote him directly, but amazon wouldn't allow such language). Nick Mason's \\"The Grand Vizier's Garden Party\\" featured his drumming along with some flute played by his then-wife, Lindy, and lots of odd sound effects.<br /><br />I rarely listen to \\"Ummagumma\\" all the way through these days, but on occasion I will listen to the live half. B001HN6918|4.0|In my opinion DreamWorks has always played second fiddle to Pixar in the computer generated feature film genre.  Their rivalry has existed for nearly as long as there has been a computer generated feature film genre! Pixar had Toy Story, DreamWorks had Small Soldiers. Pixar had A Bug's Life, DreamWorks Antz. Pixar Monsters Inc., DreamWorks Monsters Vs. Aliens.  Pixar with Cars and DreamWorks with Trucks.  No wait, I made that last one up.  But you get the idea.<br /><br />It seems to me like DreamWorks' best efforts are the ones that don't have a direct Pixar counterpart: The Shrek saga and How to Train Your Dragon to mention a few.  I am of the stance that 2008's Kung-Fu Panda perfectly represents this phenomenon of high-quality DreamWorks originality.<br /><br />What we had there was a bit of tribute to the Kung-Fu classics with a lot of unexpected character charm to cement the intense action sequences and lovely visuals.  When I heard a sequel was in the works, I naturally feared for the worst.  Generally speaking sequels in the CG film arena are hit or miss (with emphasis on miss).  Ever see Space Chimps 2, Hoodwinked 2 or Open Season 2 (or even scarier, Open Season 3)?  Sure you have your Shreks & Toy Stories but more often than not it seems to me that animation studios have a hard time recapturing the magic of the originals; especially when the original contains the ever-illusive element of heart.<br /><br />That said Kung-Fu Panda 2, riding on the success of the first one, was given a budget increase to the tune of 20-mil ($150,000,000 versus 130,000,000), brought back all of the key members of the original cast (with a few new ones) and even retained the vast majority of the production & animator crew.  To summarize, DreamWorks made darn sure not to risk losing said heart of the first film.  Did they succeed? For the most part absolutely.  Viewers looking for something more grandiose may be disappointed but the good news here is that Kung-Fu Panda 2 succeeds at feeling more like a continuation of the first film than it does a genuine sequel.  And considering the first film ended in such a manner where a sequel wasn't necessary, this is a very good thing indeed.<br /><br />Jack Black reprises his role as the titular overweight martial-arts trainee panda and his entire Furious Five retinue return uninterrupted (Tigress (Angelina Jolie), Monkey (Jackie Chan), Mantis (Seth Rogen), Viper (Lucy Liu) and Crane (David Cross)). Series staples Dustin Hoffman as Po's master Shifu and James Hong as Po's noodle-peddling dad return as well. The villain is new this time; a sociopath peacock named Shen, and played to perfection by Gary Oldman.  Other noteworthy additions include casting real-life karate aficionado Jean-Claude Van Damme to play Master Croc.<br /><br />The story takes us back to the visually lush Valley of Peace and finds a newfound camaraderie between Po and his Furious Five cohorts.  At just about the same time Po suddenly realizes that he bears (no pun intended, for now) no physical resemblance to his dad, the villainous Shen surfaces with a weapon of mass destruction and plans to conquer all of China.<br /><br />In terms of scope, the bar has been raised slightly but it's clear that the focus here was to expand upon the character back-stories and their interaction with one another over expanding geographically strictly for the sake of spending some of that additional 20-mil.<br /><br />The plot thickens slightly once Po realizes Shen's ambitions and the fact that he doesn't remember his birth parents aren't completely unrelated occurrences.  Unfortunately though, canceling out the slightly deeper plot structure of the sequel is the fact that Po's clumsy, lighthearted enthusiasm seems somehow misplaced now that he has been revealed a psychologically scarred Kung Fu disciple.<br /><br />Pacing is decent, if a bit longer feeling than the first, though all in all the 90-minute runtime does go by fairly quickly since notime need be wasted setting up backgrounds for our heroes.  Hans Zimmer nails the musical score with his trademarked near-subliminally perfect melodies and heart thumping action accompaniments.<br /><br />In all Kung-Fu Panda joins a pretty slim gathering of titles that actually manage to do the concept of a sequel justice.  While it could be argued that DreamWorks played it safe by failing to stray from the path established in the first one, at least they knew not to fix what wasn't broken.  The old phrase, \\"it's not as original as the original\\" certainly applies as well but here's hoping the formula continues to stay fresh as DreamWorks has stated they hope to milk this into a 6-movie franchise.  Will fans tire of Po's antics through four more films? There's a swamp-dwelling big green ogre who thinks not.time need be wasted setting up backgrounds for our heroes.
